> # BeOS User Interface: Technical Architecture
> 
> ## 1. The Application Framework: BApplication / BWindow / BView
> 
> ### Class Hierarchy and Messaging Backbone
> 
> The entire BeOS UI is built on a messaging architecture rooted in `BLooper` and `BHandler`.
> 
> **Inheritance chain:**
> ```
> BHandler          -- can receive BMessages
>   BLooper         -- owns a thread + message queue, dispatches to BHandlers
>     BApplication  -- singleton per process, connection to app_server
>     BWindow       -- one per window, owns its own thread
>   BView           -- attached to a BWindow, draws into it
> ```
> 
> Key design decision: `BLooper` is not just an abstract concept -- it literally spawns a kernel thread and runs a `MessageReceived()` dispatch loop on it. This means `BApplication` runs on its own thread and every `BWindow` runs on its own thread. This is the foundation of BeOS's responsiveness model.
> 
> **BApplication** is a singleton (`be_app` global). Its responsibilities:
> - Establishes connection to the `app_server` via a `BSession`/port pair
> - Runs the main message loop (but this is *not* the main thread -- `BApplication::Run()` can either spawn a thread or block the calling thread)
> - Handles app-level messages: `B_REFS_RECEIVED` (file opens), `B_ARGV_RECEIVED`, `B_READY_TO_RUN`, `B_QUIT_REQUESTED`
> - Manages the roster connection (`BRoster`) for app launching/communication
> - Provides `BApplication::Pulse()` for periodic callbacks
> 
> **BWindow** represents a window on screen. Critical details:
> - Each `BWindow` is a `BLooper`, so it has its own thread and message queue
> - Construction sends a create message to `app_server`, which returns a port pair for client-server communication
> - The window's thread runs `BWindow::DispatchMessage()`, which handles drawing, input events, and user messages
> - Window locking: `BWindow::Lock()` / `Unlock()` are required before touching any `BView` attached to it. This is a real mutex -- the window's thread holds the lock while dispatching, other threads must acquire it to interact with views
> - `UpdateIfNeeded()` triggers synchronous drawing inside the lock
> - `BWindow::Show()` both maps the window and starts its message loop thread if not already running
> 
> **BView** is the workhorse drawing/input class:
> - Views form a tree hierarchy within a window (`AddChild()`, `RemoveChild()`)
> - Each view has a frame rectangle in its parent's coordinate system and a bounds rectangle (which can be scrolled via `ScrollTo()`)
> - Views are *not* threads -- they run on their owning BWindow's thread
> - Drawing happens inside `Draw(BRect updateRect)`, which is called during the window's update cycle
> - The drawing model is immediate-mode via `app_server` protocol: calls like `StrokeLine()`, `FillRect()`, `DrawString()` send drawing commands to the server
> - Views can request drawing via `Invalidate()`, which enqueues an
>  update
> - `B_FRAME_EVENTS` flag controls whether `FrameMoved()`/`FrameResized()` are called
> - `B_WILL_DRAW` flag must be set for the view to receive `Draw()` calls
> - `B_PULSE_NEEDED` enables periodic `Pulse()` callbacks
> 
> ### The app_server Protocol
> 
> The UI is not rendered in-process. `app_server` is a separate server process (like X11, but purpose-built and much lighter). Communication happens over `BMessage`-like protocol on kernel ports. The client library (`libbe`) serializes drawing commands and sends them to `app_server`, which composites and renders to the framebuffer. This is how BeOS achieved its signature move: dragging a window showed live content, not wireframes, because `app_server` owned all the bitmaps and could blit them during moves without asking clients to redraw.
> 
> ## 2. Window Management: Tabs, Stacking, Tiling
> 
> ### The Tab-on-Top Design
> 
> BeOS windows don't have a full title bar spanning the window width. Instead, each window has a **tab** -- a small, titled protrusion at the top of the window frame. The tab width is determined by the title text length (plus close/zoom widgets). This had several consequences:
> 
> - Multiple windows could be visually adjacent at the top without their titles occluding each other
> - The tab could be positioned anywhere along the top edge of the window (it wasn't always left-aligned -- users could drag it)
> - The tab was part of the window decorator, drawn by `app_server`, not by the application
> 
> ### Window Look and Feel
> 
> BeOS used a `window_look` / `window_feel` enum system instead of a single window type:
> 
> **Looks** (visual appearance):
> - `B_TITLED_WINDOW_LOOK` -- standard tab + border
> - `B_DOCUMENT_WINDOW_LOOK` -- tab + resize corner
> - `B_MODAL_WINDOW_LOOK` -- no tab, thick border
> - `B_FLOATING_WINDOW_LOOK` -- thinner tab and border
> - `B_BORDERED_WINDOW_LOOK` -- thin border, no tab
> - `B_NO_BORDER_WINDOW_LOOK` -- frameless
> 
> **Feels** (behavioral):
> - `B_NORMAL_WINDOW_FEEL` -- standard
> - `B_MODAL_SUBSET_WINDOW_FEEL` -- modal to a subset of app windows
> - `B_MODAL_APP_WINDOW_FEEL` -- modal to entire app
> - `B_MODAL_ALL_WINDOW_FEEL` -- modal to everything
> - `B_FLOATING_SUBSET_WINDOW_FEEL` -- floats above subset
> - `B_FLOATING_APP_WINDOW_FEEL` -- floats above app windows
> - `B_FLOATING_ALL_WINDOW_FEEL` -- floats above everything
> 
> This separation of visual appearance from behavioral semantics was unusual and powerful. You could have a modal window that looked like a floating palette, or a document window that behaved as floating.
> 
> ### Stacking and Tiling (Haiku additions)
> 
> Original BeOS R5 did *not* have window stacking/tiling. Haiku added this later (circa 2011-2012, by Clemens Zeidler). The implementation:
> 
> - **Stack & Tile** uses a `SATGroup` (Stack And Tile Group) that tracks relationships between windows
> - Windows can be **stacked** (overlapping, with tabs visible side-by-side on the same title bar area, like browser tabs) or **tiled** (snapped edge-to-edge so resizing one resizes the neighbor)
> - The stacking/tiling relationships are maintained in `app_server`'s decorator layer
> - Users create stacks by dragging a window's tab near another window's tab; tiles by dragging a window edge near another window's edge
> - The constraint system uses a linear programming solver (ALM -- Auckland Layout Model) to maintain tiling relationships
> 
> ### Workspaces
> 
> BeOS supported 32 virtual workspaces, each with independent resolution and color depth settings. A window could be assigned to one or more workspaces, or to all workspaces. The `app_server` tracked per-workspace state and the workspace switcher was built into the Deskbar replicant tray.
> 
> ## 3. Tracker and Deskbar
> 
> ### Tracker
> 
> Tracker is the file manager, analogous to Finder or Explorer, but with several distinctive design decisions:
> 
> **Spatial navigation model:** Early Tracker (and through BeOS R5) used a spatial metaphor where each folder remembered its own window position, size, and view mode. Opening a folder opened a new window in its remembered position. This was a deliberate design choice shared with the classic Mac Finder.
> 
> **Live queries as virtual folders:** Tracker could display the results of BFS (Be File System) queries as if they were folders. Since BFS supported live queries (queries that update in real-time as the filesystem changes), you could have a Tracker window showing "all files modified today" that updated as you worked. This was implemented via `BQuery` objects which used the filesystem's node monitoring (`watch_node()` / `B_NODE_MONITOR` messages).
> 
> **Attribute columns:** BFS stores extended attributes natively, and Tracker could display any attribute as a sortable column. For media files, this meant artist/album/bitrate columns. For email (BeOS's mail system stored each email as a file with attributes), you'd see sender/subject/date. The attribute system was type-aware -- `B_STRING_TYPE`, `B_INT32_TYPE`, etc.
> 
> **Add-ons architecture:** Tracker supported loadable add-ons (shared libraries) that could add menu items to the context menu. Add-ons received a `BMessage` with refs to the selected files and could operate on them.
> 
> **Implementation detail:** Tracker was a single-launch app using `BApplication` with a `B_EXCLUSIVE_LAUNCH` flag (later `B_SINGLE_LAUNCH`). Each Tracker window was a `BContainerWindow` subclass containing a `BPoseView` -- "poses" being Tracker's term for file representations (icon + name).
> 
> ### Deskbar
> 
> Deskbar is the taskbar/panel, but minimal:
> 
> - **Always-visible panel** in a screen corner (configurable position)
> - **Application list:** Shows running apps with their windows. Clicking expands to show individual window list. This was populated via `BRoster` monitoring
> - **Replicant tray:** A shelf area where any BView-derived replicant could install itself (clock, workspace switcher, volume control, etc.)
> - **Be menu:** The start-menu equivalent, with app folders
> 
> Deskbar was deliberately kept simple. It was a separate application (`/boot/system/Deskbar`) that communicated with the rest of the system via standard BMessages and the roster.
> 
> **Technical implementation note:** Deskbar's tray used `BShelf` -- the same replicant hosting mechanism available to any application. This meant the tray wasn't special system infrastructure; it was just an app that happened to use the replicant system.
> 
> ## 4. Threading and Responsiveness
> 
> This is where BeOS was genuinely ahead of its time.
> 
> ### Per-Window Threading Model
> 
> Every `BWindow` runs its own `BLooper` thread. This means:
> - Window A's `Draw()` or `MessageReceived()` can block without affecting Window B
> - Input events are dispatched to the target window's thread, so a hung window doesn't block input to other windows *of the same application*
> - A slow `Draw()` in one window doesn't cause another window to appear unresponsive
> 
> Compare this to Win32 (single message pump per thread, many windows per thread), Cocoa (single main thread for all UI), or X11/GTK/Qt (single main thread for all UI). BeOS's model was the most granular.
> 
> ### Locking Protocol
> 
> The cost of per-window threads: you need locking discipline.
> 
> - To modify any `BView`, you must hold its owning `BWindow`'s lock
> - `BWindow::Lock()` acquires the lock; the window's own thread holds it during message dispatch
> - If the window thread is in `Draw()` or `MessageReceived()`, you can access views freely (you're already on the right thread with the lock held)
> - From *other* threads, you must `Lock()` the window, modify views, then `Unlock()`
> - `BWindow::LockWithTimeout()` prevents deadlocks in complex multi-window scenarios
> 
> This was a source of bugs for new developers (use-after-delete if the window quit while you were waiting for the lock, race conditions if you forgot to lock), but the model itself was sound.
> 
> ### BMessageRunner and Asynchronous Design
> 
> BeOS heavily favored asynchronous message-passing over synchronous calls:
> - `BMessenger` can target any `BHandler` in any `BLooper`, same or different application
> - `BInvoker` provides a callback-via-message pattern
> - `BMessageRunner` sends periodic messages to a target (preferred over timers)
> - Inter-app communication used the same `BMessage` infrastructure, just routed through kernel ports
> 
> ### The app_server Thread Architecture
> 
> `app_server` itself was multi-threaded:
> - One thread per client app connection
> - A dedicated drawing/compositing thread
> - Input server (`input_server`) was a separate process that generated raw input events, which `app_server` dispatched to the correct window
> 
> ## 5. The Replicant System
> 
> Replicants are one of BeOS's most distinctive features -- live, functioning UI components that can be dragged from one application and embedded in another.
> 
> ### How It Works
> 
> A replicant is any `BView` subclass that:
> 1. Has a static `Instantiate(BMessage* archive)` method
> 2. Can serialize itself to a `BMessage` via `Archive()` (inherited from `BArchivable`)
> 3. Lives in a shared library (add-on) or a running app
> 
> The hosting side uses `BShelf`, which is a `BView` that can accept replicant drops:
> - `BShelf` monitors for `B_ARCHIVED_OBJECT` messages dropped onto it
> - On drop, it calls `instantiate_object()` which looks up the class by name in the message's `class` field
> - The runtime loads the appropriate add-on (the archive stores the app signature and add-on path) and calls `Instantiate()`
> - The instantiated `BView` is added as a child of the shelf
> 
> ### What This Means in Practice
> 
> - The ActivityMonitor's CPU graph could be dragged onto the Desktop (Tracker's desktop was a `BShelf`)
> - A clock widget from one app could live inside another app's window
> - Deskbar's tray items were replicants
> - The replicant retained its full functionality -- it was a live `BView` running actual code, not a screenshot or static copy
> 
> ### Technical Constraints
> 
> - The replicant's code must be accessible (in a shared library, or the originating app must still be running)
> - The archive `BMessage` must contain everything needed to reconstruct the view (the `Archive()` contract)
> - Replicants run in the *host* process's address space, on the host window's thread -- there was a trust model issue here
> - Each replicant gets a small resize handle (a `BDragger`) to allow re-dragging or removal
> 
> ### The Archive/Instantiate Protocol
> 
> `BArchivable` provides the serialization backbone:
> ```cpp
> // Archiving (serializing)
> status_t MyView::Archive(BMessage* into, bool deep) const {
>     BView::Archive(into, deep);  // parent archives class name, frame, etc.
>     into->AddString("mydata", fSomeState);
>     return B_OK;
> }
> 
> // Instantiation (deserializing)
> BArchivable* MyView::Instantiate(BMessage* archive) {
>     if (!validate_instantiation(archive, "MyView"))
>         return NULL;
>     return new MyView(archive);
> }
> ```
> 
> The `class` field in the archive stores the C++ class name. `instantiate_object()` uses a global function map (`BArchivable`'s static registry, plus add-on loading) to find the right `Instantiate()` function.
> 
> ## 6. Typography and Rendering
> 
> ### Font Architecture
> 
> BeOS used its own font rendering system, not FreeType (at least in the Be Inc. releases -- Haiku switched to FreeType2).
> 
> - `app_server` managed all font rendering centrally
> - Fonts were rasterized server-side; clients specified fonts via `BFont` objects that stored family, style, size, shear, rotation, spacing, and encoding
> - `BFont` supported:
>   - Antialiased rendering (notable for the era -- this was mid-1990s)
>   - Fractional font metrics and positioning
>   - Unicode support (though incomplete in R5)
>   - Font overlays for missing glyphs
> 
> ### Drawing Model
> 
> BeOS used a stateful drawing model in `BView`:
> - `SetHighColor()` / `SetLowColor()` -- foreground/background colors
> - `SetDrawingMode()` -- compositing modes: `B_OP_COPY`, `B_OP_OVER` (alpha-aware), `B_OP_BLEND`, `B_OP_ERASE`, `B_OP_INVERT`, `B_OP_SELECT`, etc.
> - `SetPenSize()` for stroke width
> - Pattern-based fills using 8x8 bit patterns (`pattern` struct)
> - Drawing commands: `StrokeLine`, `StrokeRect`, `FillRect`, `StrokeEllipse`, `FillEllipse`, `StrokeBezier`, `DrawString`, `DrawBitmap`, `StrokePolygon`, etc.
> 
> All drawing calls were serialized and sent to `app_server` over the port connection. `app_server` maintained a per-view state (colors, pen, font, clipping region) and executed the drawing commands against the window's server-side bitmap.
> 
> ### Clipping and Invalidation
> 
> - Each `BView` had a clipping region maintained by `app_server`, automatically intersected with the view's visible area (accounting for overlapping sibling views and the window's position)
> - `Invalidate(BRect)` or `Invalidate(BRegion)` queued an update
> - During the update cycle, `app_server` would call back with `B_DRAW` messages, and the window's thread would invoke each dirty view's `Draw()` with the update rect
> - `BView::BeginRectTracking()` / `EndRectTracking()` provided rubber-band selection
> - `BPicture` allowed recording drawing commands for later playback
> 
> ### Haiku's Rendering Improvements
> 
> Haiku replaced the original font engine with FreeType2 and added:
> - Subpixel antialiasing (LCD-optimized rendering)
> - Alpha-channel compositing (the original BeOS had limited alpha support)
> - Hardware-accelerated drawing where available (though much rendering remained software)
> - `BLayout` classes for automatic view layout (absent in original BeOS)
> 
> ## 7. Terminal
> 
> BeOS's Terminal (`/boot/apps/Terminal` in R5, `/boot/system/apps/Terminal` in Haiku) had several interesting properties:
> 
> ### Implementation Details
> 
> - Terminal was a standard BeOS application using `BApplication` + `BWindow` + custom `BView`
> - The terminal emulator view (`TermView` in Haiku) rendered text directly using `BView::DrawString()` with a fixed-width font
> - PTY (pseudo-terminal) handling used POSIX `openpty()`/`forkpty()` or the equivalent Be-specific API
> - Each terminal window ran the shell in a child process, with I/O mediated by the window's thread reading from the PTY fd
> 
> ### Notable Features
> 
> - **Multiple shells per window:** Haiku's Terminal (not sure about original BeOS R5) supported tabs before most desktop terminal emulators did
> - **Hyperlinks:** Haiku's Terminal recognizes URLs in output and allows clicking them
> - **Drag & drop:** Text selection could be dragged out (as text), and files could be dragged onto the terminal (path inserted at cursor)
> - **Scrollback** maintained in a ring buffer
> - **Replicant support:** The terminal view could be embedded as a replicant in other applications (you could drag a functioning terminal onto your desktop)
> - **Color scheme support** and configurable fonts
> - **UTF-8 support:** BeOS standardized on UTF-8 as the system encoding, so Terminal was UTF-8 native -- unusual for late-1990s terminal emulators
> 
> ### Threading Interaction
> 
> Terminal is a good case study of the per-window thread model:
> - The window thread handles drawing and input events
> - A separate thread (or select/poll on the PTY fd) reads shell output
> - Shell output is parsed for ANSI escape sequences, the terminal state (cursor position, attributes, scrollback) is updated, and `Invalidate()` is called to trigger redraw
> - The window lock must be acquired when updating the terminal buffer if it's accessed from both the reader thread and the drawing thread
> 
> ### Shell Integration
> 
> BeOS shipped with `bash` as the default shell. The terminal supported standard `TERM` types (typically `beterm` or `xterm`). BeOS's POSIX layer was incomplete in some areas, so terminal applications that relied on unusual ioctl calls or termios features could hit gaps, but basic terminal semantics worked.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Design Philosophy Summary
> 
> The through-line of BeOS's UI architecture is **pervasive multithreading with message-passing isolation**:
> 
> - Every window is a concurrent actor with its own message queue
> - Communication between actors uses `BMessage` (a typed key-value bag, essentially a dictionary)
> - The display server (`app_server`) is a separate process, isolating rendering from application crashes
> - The replicant system extends the message-passing/archival model to allow live UI embedding
> - The window decorator is server-side, so window chrome remains responsive even when the application is hung (the server can still draw the frame and title, and the close button can send a quit message)
> 
> The weaknesses of the model are also clear: the locking discipline is error-prone (no ownership types, no borrow checker), the replicant system has no sandboxing (embedded code runs with host privileges), and the per-window thread model means an app with many windows (like Tracker) creates many threads.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Key references for deeper exploration:**
> - Haiku source: `https://github.com/haiku/haiku` -- particularly `src/kits/interface/`, `src/servers/app/`, `src/apps/terminal/`, `src/apps/tracker/`, `src/apps/deskbar/`
> - The Be Book (archived): `https://www.haiku-os.org/legacy-docs/bebook/` -- the original API documentation
> - Haiku API docs: `https://www.haiku-os.org/docs/api/`
> - Haiku's Stack & Tile: `src/servers/app/stackandtile/` in the Haiku repo
> - Ingo Weinhold and Axel Dorfler's design documents in the Haiku trac wiki
> - "Programming the Be Operating System" by Dan Parks Sydow (O'Reilly, 1999) -- the best book-length treatment of the API</result>

> 
> # Tiling Window Manager Architecture: Technical Research
> 
> ## 1. i3/sway — Tree-Based Container Model
> 
> ### Container Tree Architecture
> 
> i3 (and its Wayland port, sway) organize windows in a **tree of containers**. Every workspace is the root of an n-ary tree where each node is a container that can be:
> 
> - A **leaf** (holding a single window/surface)
> - A **split container** (horizontal or vertical, holding child containers)
> - A **tabbed** or **stacked** container (children rendered as tabs or stacked title bars)
> 
> This is the key architectural insight: layout is not a predefined algorithm applied to a flat list of windows — it is an emergent property of the tree structure itself. Users manipulate the tree directly (split, move, reparent), and the geometry falls out of recursive subdivision. The tree is persistent across operations; moving a window means reparenting a node, not recalculating an entire layout.
> 
> Source: i3 docs — https://i3wm.org/docs/userguide.html#_tree / sway source at https://github.com/swaywm/sway
> 
> ### IPC Protocol
> 
> i3/sway expose a **Unix domain socket IPC** using a simple binary protocol:
> 
> - Header: magic bytes `i3-ipc`, followed by a 32-bit payload length and 32-bit message type (both little-endian)
> - Payload: JSON
> - Message types include: `RUN_COMMAND`, `GET_WORKSPACES`, `SUBSCRIBE`, `GET_TREE`, `GET_MARKS`, `GET_BAR_CONFIG`, `GET_BINDING_MODES`, `GET_CONFIG`, `GET_INPUTS`, `GET_SEATS` (sway extensions)
> - **Event subscription** model: clients send a `SUBSCRIBE` message with a list of event types (workspace, window, binding, shutdown, etc.), then receive async JSON events over the same socket
> 
> `GET_TREE` returns the **entire container tree** as a nested JSON object — this is what tools like `i3-msg -t get_tree` use. Each node has fields for geometry (`rect`), type (`root`, `output`, `workspace`, `con`, `floating_con`), layout (`splith`, `splitv`, `tabbed`, `stacked`), and child arrays.
> 
> Sway maintains wire compatibility with i3's IPC but extends it for Wayland-specific concepts (inputs, seats). Tools like `swayipc` (Python), `i3ipc-go`, and `i3ipc-rs` wrap this protocol.
> 
> Source: https://i3wm.org/docs/ipc.html
> 
> ### Mixed Tiling/Floating
> 
> Each workspace maintains **two separate child lists**: a tiled container tree and a `floating_nodes` list. Floating windows:
> 
> - Are rendered above tiled windows in the stacking order
> - Have free-form geometry (user-draggable/resizable)
> - Can be toggled between tiled and floating with `floating toggle`
> - When toggled back to tiling, the window is inserted into the tree at the current focus point
> 
> Certain windows (dialogs, popups) are auto-floated based on window type hints (`_NET_WM_WINDOW_TYPE` on X11, or xdg-toplevel states on Wayland). This is configured via `for_window` rules.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2. dwm — Master-Stack Layout Philosophy
> 
> ### Architecture
> 
> dwm is deliberately minimal (~2000 lines of C, single C file by design philosophy). It is configured by editing `config.h` and recompiling — there is no runtime config file or IPC. This is a philosophical statement: the WM is a personal tool you compile for yourself.
> 
> Source: https://dwm.suckless.org/ / https://git.suckless.org/dwm/
> 
> ### Layout Model
> 
> dwm maintains a **flat linked list of clients** per monitor. Layouts are functions that receive the list and assign geometries. The three default layouts are:
> 
> - **Tile**: one master area (left, configurable ratio via `mfact`) + stack area (right). `nmaster` controls how many windows occupy the master area. This is the core idea — you have one or a few primary windows you're actively working with, and the rest are visible but secondary.
> - **Monocle**: all windows fullscreen, overlapping (effectively a tab switcher)
> - **Float**: free-form, like a conventional WM
> 
> Layouts are hot-swappable per-tag. The layout function signature is simply `void (*arrange)(Monitor *)` — you can write custom layouts as patches. The community has produced dozens: fibonacci, grid, deck, centeredmaster, bottomstack, etc.
> 
> ### Tagging vs. Workspaces
> 
> This is dwm's most distinctive design choice. Instead of workspaces (mutually exclusive sets), dwm uses a **bitmask tagging system**:
> 
> - There are 9 tags (bits 0–8 of a `unsigned int`)
> - Each client has a tag bitmask (can belong to multiple tags simultaneously)
> - Each monitor has a **view bitmask** (can display multiple tags simultaneously)
> - A client is visible iff `(client->tags & monitor->tagset) != 0`
> 
> This means you can:
> - View tags 1+3 simultaneously (bitwise OR of their masks)
> - Assign a window to tags 2+5 (it appears whenever either is viewed)
> - "Sticky" windows belong to all tags (`tags = ~0`)
> 
> This is strictly more expressive than workspaces. The cost is conceptual complexity — most users end up treating tags as workspaces anyway.
> 
> Source: https://dwm.suckless.org/tutorial/
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3. bspwm — Binary Space Partitioning
> 
> ### BSP Tree Model
> 
> bspwm represents the layout as a **full binary tree** where:
> 
> - **Internal nodes** are splits (horizontal or vertical, with a configurable ratio)
> - **Leaf nodes** are windows (or empty/receptacles)
> 
> Every window insertion performs a binary split on the currently focused leaf. The split direction alternates (or follows a scheme: `automatic`, `longest_side`, `spiral`). This produces a recursive bisection of screen space.
> 
> The critical difference from i3's tree: i3 allows n-ary trees (a container can have any number of children), while bspwm strictly maintains a binary tree. This constrains the layout space but makes the data structure more uniform and predictable.
> 
> Source: https://github.com/baskerville/bspwm / the man page `bspwm(1)`
> 
> ### bspc — External Control
> 
> bspwm is controlled entirely through an external client, `bspc`, which communicates over a Unix socket. The WM itself does not handle keybindings — that's delegated to `sxhkd` (simple X hotkey daemon), a separate process. This is a strong separation-of-concerns design.
> 
> `bspc` commands operate on a **domain-specific query language** for selecting nodes:
> 
> ```
> bspc node [NODE_SEL] -s [NODE_SEL]    # swap two nodes
> bspc node -p east                       # preselect split direction
> bspc query -N -n .focused.local.!hidden # query nodes matching selectors
> ```
> 
> Node selectors use path syntax and descriptor flags: `.focused`, `.local`, `.floating`, `.tiled`, `.leaf`, `.window`, direction modifiers (`east`, `west`, `north`, `south`), and tree-traversal modifiers (`@parent`, `@brother`, `@first`, `@second`).
> 
> ### Receptacles and Preselection
> 
> Two novel features:
> 
> - **Preselection**: you can mark where the *next* window will be inserted before it appears, including the split direction and ratio. This gives spatial intentionality to window placement.
> - **Receptacles**: empty leaf nodes that act as placeholders. You can create a layout with empty slots, then have windows fill them (matched by rules). This enables templated layouts.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4. Hyprland — Dynamic Tiling with Animations
> 
> ### Architecture
> 
> Hyprland is a Wayland compositor (not just a WM — on Wayland, the compositor *is* the WM + display server). Written in C++, it directly implements the `wlroots` library protocols.
> 
> Source: https://hyprland.org/ / https://github.com/hyprwm/Hyprland
> 
> ### Layout and Animation
> 
> Hyprland's default layout is **dwindle** (a variant of binary space partitioning, similar to bspwm), with an alternative **master** layout. The key differentiator is that all layout transitions are **animated**:
> 
> - Window open/close: fade, slide, or custom bezier-curve animations
> - Window move/resize: smooth interpolation between geometries
> - Workspace switch: slide/fade transitions
> - Border colors animate on focus change
> 
> Animations use configurable bezier curves (defined by control points). The rendering pipeline uses OpenGL ES for compositing, enabling:
> 
> - Per-window opacity and blur (kawase blur, dual-pass)
> - Window rounding (rounded corners with configurable radius)
> - Drop shadows
> - Layer-based rendering with explicit ordering
> 
> ### Dynamic Tiling
> 
> "Dynamic" means windows can be freely dragged/resized while in tiling mode — the layout adjusts in real-time rather than snapping. Windows can be moved within the tiling tree by dragging them over other windows (the tree restructures live). This blurs the tiling/floating boundary more aggressively than i3.
> 
> ### Configuration
> 
> Runtime-reloadable config file (`hyprland.conf`) with a custom syntax. Also exposes an IPC socket (JSON protocol) and supports plugins via a C++ API (shared libraries loaded at runtime).
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 5. river and niri — Wayland-Native Novel Approaches
> 
> ### river
> 
> river is a Wayland compositor written in **Zig**, using wlroots. Its distinctive feature is **stackable layout generators as external processes**.
> 
> Source: https://github.com/riverwm/river / https://isaacfreund.com/blog/river-intro/
> 
> The WM itself contains **no layout logic**. Instead:
> 
> 1. river sends layout demands to an external layout generator process via a Wayland protocol extension (`river-layout-v3`)
> 2. The generator responds with window geometries
> 3. This means layouts are fully pluggable — any program speaking the protocol can define layouts
> 4. Default generator is `rivertile`, but users can write generators in any language
> 
> This is arguably the cleanest separation of layout logic from window management. It means you can write a layout in Python, Rust, Zig, a shell script — whatever speaks the protocol.
> 
> river also uses dwm-style **tags** (bitmask, not workspaces) and has a strong focus on keyboard-driven operation.
> 
> ### niri
> 
> niri is a **scrolling tiling** Wayland compositor, written in Rust. This is the genuinely novel contribution.
> 
> Source: https://github.com/YaLTeR/niri
> 
> The core idea: windows are arranged in a **single horizontal strip** that extends infinitely. You scroll left/right through the strip. Each column contains one or more windows stacked vertically. This is inspired by PaperWM (a GNOME Shell extension) but implemented as a standalone compositor.
> 
> Key design decisions:
> 
> - **No traditional workspaces**: the strip *is* the workspace. Windows to the left/right of the viewport exist but aren't visible.
> - **Columns** can contain one window (full height) or be split vertically
> - **Animated scrolling** between focus targets (the viewport follows focus)
> - Windows have a configured **default width** (fraction of screen or fixed pixels), so new windows don't squish existing ones
> - **Overview mode**: zooms out to show all windows in the strip
> 
> This model eliminates the "where do I put this window" problem — new windows just go to the right of the current focus, extending the strip. It trades spatial layout flexibility for navigational simplicity. It works very well for the common case of "I have N things and I look at 1-2 at a time."
> 
> niri also provides screenshot-to-screencast-as-a-protocol support and window-close animations.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 6. Peripheral Integration: Bars, Notifications, Launchers, Terminals
> 
> ### Status Bars
> 
> Two dominant architectural approaches:
> 
> **Protocol-based (i3bar protocol):** i3/sway define a **JSON streaming protocol** for status bars. The bar process reads JSON objects from a status generator's stdout, each containing an array of "blocks" with text, color, markup, click-event handlers. Status generators (`i3status`, `i3status-rs`, `i3blocks`) produce these JSON lines. The bar renders them. This separates content generation from rendering.
> 
> Source: https://i3wm.org/docs/i3bar-protocol.html
> 
> **Wayland layer-shell:** On Wayland, bars use the `wlr-layer-shell` protocol to position themselves as non-interactive (or selectively interactive) layers above or below windows. `waybar` is the dominant implementation — it's a standalone process that:
> - Queries WM state via IPC (sway, Hyprland, river all supported)
> - Renders modules (clock, workspaces, system tray, custom scripts)
> - Styled via CSS (yes, actual CSS with selectors and properties)
> 
> **dwm's built-in bar:** dwm draws its own bar using Xlib directly. The bar content from external sources is fed via the root window name (`xsetroot -name`). This is maximally simple but inflexible.
> 
> **Polybar, eww, ags:** Polybar (X11) and eww/ags (Wayland) represent the "make the bar a full widget system" approach. eww (Elkowar's Wacky Widgets) uses a custom markup language (yuck) and CSS for styling. ags (Aylur's GTK Shell) uses JavaScript/TypeScript with GTK — essentially a scripted desktop shell.
> 
> ### Notifications
> 
> Most tiling WMs delegate notifications to a standalone daemon implementing the `org.freedesktop.Notifications` D-Bus interface:
> 
> - **dunst**: the classic. Configurable rules for filtering/styling notifications. On Wayland, uses layer-shell.
> - **mako**: Wayland-native, minimal, uses layer-shell
> - **fnott**: another Wayland-native option, Fcft-based rendering
> - **swaync**: sway notification center — adds a notification panel/history (like GNOME's)
> 
> The D-Bus interface is the key abstraction — any application calling `notify-send` or using `libnotify` works with any of these daemons.
> 
> ### Application Launchers
> 
> - **dmenu**: the original. Reads newline-delimited items from stdin, presents a menu bar, outputs the selected item to stdout. Unix philosophy: it's a general-purpose selector, not specifically an app launcher.
> - **rofi**: dmenu replacement with modes (drun for .desktop files, window switcher, ssh, custom scripts). X11-native, has a Wayland fork.
> - **fuzzel**: Wayland-native dmenu/rofi alternative using layer-shell. Renders with client-side OpenGL.
> - **wofi**: GTK-based Wayland launcher
> - **tofi**: extremely minimal Wayland launcher (harfbuzz + vulkan)
> 
> The architectural pattern: all of these are **standalone processes** that the WM launches on a keybinding. They communicate the result (selected application) by simply executing it. No WM integration needed beyond "run this command on this key."
> 
> ### Terminal Emulator Integration
> 
> Tiling WMs don't treat terminals specially — they're just windows. But the choice of terminal matters for performance:
> 
> - **foot**: Wayland-native, written in C. Notable for its **PGO (profile-guided optimization)** build process and **sixel** support. Uses client-side rendering with a custom font rasterizer (fcft). Designed for speed on Wayland — no X11 support at all.
> - **alacritty**: Cross-platform, Rust, GPU-accelerated via OpenGL. No tabs, no splits — delegates those to the WM or a multiplexer. This is a philosophical alignment with tiling WMs: the terminal doesn't need to tile because the WM already does.
> - **kitty**: has its own **graphics protocol** (see section 8), tabs, layouts, and IPC. More of a platform than a terminal.
> - **wezterm**: Rust, GPU-rendered, has multiplexing and a Lua configuration/scripting layer.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 7. Scratchpad Windows
> 
> The scratchpad concept bridges tiling and floating. Implementations vary but the core idea is consistent:
> 
> ### i3/sway
> 
> A special workspace called `__i3_scratch` that is never directly viewed. Windows moved to the scratchpad are:
> 1. Removed from the visible tiling tree
> 2. Set to floating mode
> 3. Hidden
> 
> `scratchpad show` toggles the most recently used scratchpad window: it appears centered and floating over the current workspace. If it's already visible, it hides again.
> 
> ```
> # Typical usage:
> bindsym $mod+Shift+minus move scratchpad
> bindsym $mod+minus scratchpad show
> ```
> 
> You can have multiple scratchpad windows and cycle through them. Criteria-based show commands (`[app_id="spotify"] scratchpad show`) let you toggle specific applications, effectively creating **named scratchpads** — persistent floating windows you summon and dismiss.
> 
> ### Hyprland
> 
> Hyprland has `special` workspaces (functionally equivalent to scratchpads). They can be named, toggled per-monitor, and animated. Windows in special workspaces can be viewed with a slide-from-top animation.
> 
> ### dwm
> 
> dwm's scratchpad is a common **patch** (not built-in). The typical implementation uses a dedicated tag bit for the scratchpad and toggles its visibility. Since dwm tags are bitmasks, this is trivial: toggle one bit in the view mask.
> 
> ### Broader Pattern
> 
> Scratchpads are essentially **named, toggle-able floating layers**. They solve the problem of "I need quick access to X (calculator, music player, terminal) but don't want it taking tiling space permanently." The abstraction is: a window that exists in a hidden state and can be summoned/dismissed with a single key, always appearing floating and centered regardless of the current tiling layout.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 8. Terminal-as-First-Class-Citizen: Graphics and Architecture
> 
> ### Kitty Graphics Protocol
> 
> Kitty's terminal graphics protocol is the most ambitious attempt to make graphical content native in terminals.
> 
> Source: https://sw.kovidgoyal.net/kitty/graphics-protocol/
> 
> How it works:
> - Images are transmitted to the terminal via **escape sequences** embedded in the output stream
> - Transmission methods: **direct** (base64-encoded pixel data in the escape sequence), **file** (pass a file path or fd), **shared memory** (POSIX shm for zero-copy)
> - Images are assigned **IDs** and stored by the terminal. They can then be **placed** at specific cell positions with a separate escape sequence.
> - Placements support: z-index ordering, cell-offset positioning, scaling, cropping
> - **Animations**: the protocol supports multi-frame images with per-frame timing
> - **Unicode placeholder mode**: uses a special Unicode character (U+10EEEE) to represent image cells. This means the image occupies "real" cells in the terminal grid, so screen/tmux-style multiplexers can theoretically preserve them across redraws.
> 
> Adoption: kitty (native), WezTerm (supported), Ghostty (supported), Konsole (partial). Notably **not** supported in alacritty (philosophical opposition to complexity) or foot (maintainer prefers sixel).
> 
> ### Sixel Graphics
> 
> An older protocol (originally from DEC terminals, 1980s) that encodes raster images as character data. Supported by foot, xterm, mlterm, and others. Lower capability than kitty's protocol (no animation, no z-index, no virtual placements), but more widely implemented.
> 
> ### Alacritty's GPU Architecture
> 
> Alacritty renders the terminal grid as **textured quads on the GPU** using OpenGL:
> - Each cell is a quad with a glyph texture from a font atlas
> - Font rasterization happens on CPU (via freetype/fontconfig), results are cached in a GPU texture atlas
> - The entire visible grid is re-rendered each frame (no dirty-rectangle tracking in the original design, though this has evolved)
> - This means scrolling, cursor movement, and text updates are all just "render the grid" — no complex damage tracking
> 
> Source: https://github.com/alacritty/alacritty
> 
> ### foot's Architecture
> 
> foot uses a different approach optimized for **Wayland's damage-tracking model**:
> - Client-side rendering to shared-memory buffers (wl_shm), not GPU
> - Tracks damage (which cells changed) and only redraws dirty regions
> - Submits damage rects to the Wayland compositor, which can then optimize compositing
> - Uses fcft for font rendering (a purpose-built font library)
> - Achieves competitive performance to GPU-rendered terminals because Wayland compositors can handle the compositing efficiently
> 
> Source: https://codeberg.org/dnkl/foot
> 
> ### Ghostty
> 
> Worth mentioning: Ghostty (by Mitchell Hashimoto) is a newer GPU-accelerated terminal using platform-native rendering (Metal on macOS, OpenGL on Linux). It supports the kitty graphics protocol, has a libghostty core library, and embeds as a library for integration into other applications.
> 
> Source: https://ghostty.org/ / https://github.com/ghostty-org/ghostty
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 9. Hybrid TUI/GUI Approaches
> 
> ### notcurses
> 
> notcurses (by Nick Black) is a C library for building terminal UIs that explicitly targets **multimedia in the terminal**:
> 
> - Supports sixel, kitty graphics protocol, and iterm2 inline images — auto-detecting the best available method
> - Renders images, video (via ffmpeg integration), and 3D content within the terminal grid
> - Provides a **plane** abstraction: multiple overlapping rendering surfaces with z-ordering (essentially a compositing model within the terminal)
> - Handles input, unicode, cell-level styling, and animations
> 
> This is the most serious attempt at making the terminal a general-purpose rendering surface. The philosophical position: the terminal is a better UI platform than most people realize, and the missing piece was graphics, not interactivity.
> 
> Source: https://nick-black.com/dankwiki/index.php/Notcurses / https://github.com/dankamongmen/notcurses
> 
> ### Textual (by Textualize / Will McGuigan)
> 
> Textual is a Python framework for building TUI applications, built on top of Rich:
> 
> - **CSS-based layout**: Textual uses an actual CSS subset for widget layout (flexbox, grid, docking). Widgets have CSS classes and IDs. This is significant — it brings web-layout semantics to the terminal.
> - **Widget tree**: a DOM-like tree of widgets, with event bubbling, focus management, and reactive data binding
> - **Async-first**: built on Python's asyncio, with message passing between widgets
> - **Snapshot testing**: can capture terminal output as SVG for visual regression tests
> - Ships with a **devtools** system (live CSS editing, DOM inspector) — essentially browser devtools for the terminal
> 
> Source: https://textual.textualize.io/ / https://github.com/Textualize/textual
> 
> ### Zellij
> 
> Zellij is a terminal multiplexer (tmux/screen replacement) written in Rust that pushes toward being a **terminal-native tiling WM**:
> 
> - Plugin system using **WebAssembly**: plugins run in a WASM sandbox with a defined API for interacting with the multiplexer
> - Layout system with configurable panes (tiling within the terminal, mirroring what a WM does at the desktop level)
> - Built-in UI elements: tab bar, status bar, session manager
> - Straddling mode: acts as both a multiplexer and a rudimentary WM for terminal applications
> 
> Source: https://zellij.dev/ / https://github.com/zellij-org/zellij
> 
> ### Other Hybrid Approaches
> 
> - **Browsh**: a text-based browser that runs a headless Firefox and renders the visual output as text/sixel in a terminal. This is the extreme end of "GUI app in terminal."
> - **Neovide**: Neovim frontend with GPU-rendered smooth scrolling and cursor animations — taking a TUI app (neovim) and giving it GUI polish while keeping the terminal-paradigm interaction model.
> - **Yazi/lf/ranger**: terminal file managers that integrate with kitty graphics protocol or sixel for image previews, bridging file management (traditionally GUI) into the terminal.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Summary of Architectural Patterns
> 
> The design space has a few clear axes:
> 
> | Axis | Spectrum |
> |------|----------|
> | Layout model | Flat list + algorithm (dwm) ↔ User-manipulated tree (i3) ↔ External generator (river) |
> | Configuration | Compile-time (dwm) ↔ Config file (i3/sway) ↔ Scripting engine (Hyprland plugins, ags) |
> | Separation of concerns | Monolithic (dwm) ↔ Modular (bspwm+sxhkd+bar+notif) |
> | Workspace model | Tags/bitmask (dwm, river) ↔ Named workspaces (i3) ↔ Infinite strip (niri) |
> | Tiling/floating boundary | Hard separation (i3) ↔ Dynamic/blurred (Hyprland) |
> | Terminal as platform | Pure text (traditional) ↔ Graphics-capable surface (kitty, notcurses) |
> 
> The clear trend in the Wayland generation is: compositors as integrated systems (since Wayland merges the display server and WM roles), with standardized protocols (layer-shell, foreign-toplevel, ext-session-lock) replacing X11's ad-hoc mechanisms. The cost is that each compositor reimplements more functionality; the benefit is better integration and fewer protocol races.</result>

> # Plan 9 from Bell Labs: Windowing, Text-as-Interface, and Filesystem-as-IPC
> 
> ## 1. 8½ and rio: The Window System as File Server
> 
> ### 8½ (Rob Pike, 1991)
> 
> 8½ was Plan 9's original window system, described in Pike's paper "8½, the Plan 9 Window System" (1991). The core insight: **a window system is a file server that multiplexes access to a terminal's I/O devices**.
> 
> Each window in 8½ is a complete, independent environment. When a process is started in a window, it sees these files as if it were running on a dedicated terminal:
> 
> - `/dev/cons` — the text console (reads yield keyboard input, writes produce text output)
> - `/dev/mouse` — mouse events (position, button state)
> - `/dev/bitblt` — graphics operations (later replaced by `/dev/draw`)
> - `/dev/screen` — the full display
> - `/dev/window` — the window's own rectangle (position and size)
> 
> The critical mechanism: **per-process namespaces**. Plan 9 doesn't have a single global filesystem namespace. Each process has its own namespace, constructed by bind and mount operations. When 8½ creates a new window, it:
> 
> 1. Forks the namespace
> 2. Mounts its own file server over `/dev`, replacing the parent's `/dev/cons`, `/dev/mouse`, etc. with synthetic files specific to the new window
> 3. Starts the shell (rc) in this new namespace
> 
> So a process reading `/dev/cons` gets input from *its* window's keyboard focus. A process reading `/dev/mouse` gets events from *its* window's mouse. The process has no idea it's in a window -- it sees the same interface a process on a raw terminal would see. This is multiplexing by namespace manipulation, not by API.
> 
> 8½ itself is simultaneously:
> - A **client** of the real terminal devices (it reads the real `/dev/mouse`, draws to the real `/dev/bitblt`)
> - A **file server** that synthesizes per-window virtual devices
> - A **window manager** that handles overlapping, focus, menus
> 
> ### rio (Russ Cox, ~2000, replacing 8½ in later Plan 9 releases)
> 
> rio refined 8½ with a cleaner implementation but the same philosophy. Key differences:
> 
> - Uses `/dev/draw` (the newer graphics device) instead of `/dev/bitblt`
> - Exposes `/dev/winname` to identify windows
> - The window server speaks 9P (Plan 9's file protocol), so it can serve windows to remote machines transparently
> - Simpler codebase, better integration with Plan 9's evolved namespace conventions
> 
> rio exposes per-window:
> - `/dev/cons` — text I/O
> - `/dev/consctl` — console control (e.g., `rawon` to get raw characters)
> - `/dev/mouse` — mouse position and buttons
> - `/dev/cursor` — set the cursor image
> - `/dev/draw` — graphics interface
> - `/dev/window` — window geometry (readable as `minx miny maxx maxy`)
> - `/dev/wctl` — window control (resize, move, hide, current, top, bottom, delete)
> - `/dev/wsys` — creating new windows (writing "new" to it)
> - `/dev/label` — window title/label
> - `/dev/snarf` — clipboard (read to paste, write to copy)
> 
> To create a new window programmatically: `mount /srv/rio /mnt/wsys new` then read the files in `/mnt/wsys` to interact with it. Or write to `/dev/wsys`. The window itself is just a filesystem.
> 
> **Recursive windowing**: Since rio is just a program that reads `/dev/mouse` and `/dev/draw` and serves new synthetic `/dev/mouse` and `/dev/draw`, you can run rio inside rio. Each nested rio thinks it's talking to the real terminal -- because from its namespace perspective, it is. This falls out of the design for free.
> 
> ## 2. Text-Centric UI Philosophy
> 
> Plan 9's fundamental position: **text is the universal interface**. This goes deeper than Unix's "everything is a stream of bytes":
> 
> - In Unix, text flows through pipes but GUIs are a separate world (X11, Wayland -- protocol-based, not file-based).
> - In Plan 9, the GUI *is* text. Windows are files. Mouse events are text-formatted data. Graphics commands are structured byte streams through files. There is no separate "GUI toolkit" paradigm.
> 
> The philosophy manifests at every level:
> 
> **Console model**: Every rio window is fundamentally a text terminal with a scrollback buffer. You interact with it by typing text and reading text. The shell prompt, command output, and your edits are all in the same text buffer, which is fully editable -- you can go back, edit a previous command in place, and re-execute it by clicking.
> 
> **Text as action**: In rio, you can select any text in any window and execute it. Button 2 (middle click) executes the selection as a command. Button 3 (right click) performs a "plumb" or "look" action on the selected text. This means any visible text can be a command or a reference -- the UI is literally made of text that does things.
> 
> **No widgets**: Plan 9 doesn't have button widgets, dropdown menus, dialog boxes (in the conventional sense). Instead, you have text that you click on to make things happen. The tag line in acme windows is the most refined version of this -- a line of text where each word is a clickable command.
> 
> ## 3. /dev/draw and /dev/cons Interfaces
> 
> ### /dev/draw
> 
> `/dev/draw` is the graphics device, served by the kernel (or by the window system for windowed processes). It implements a 2D imaging model inspired by Porter-Duff compositing. The interface is:
> 
> - `/dev/draw/new` — allocate a new connection (returns a connection ID)
> - `/dev/draw/N/ctl` — control file for connection N (read to get image parameters)
> - `/dev/draw/N/data` — write drawing commands as binary-encoded messages
> - `/dev/draw/N/refresh` — read to learn which regions need redrawing (after window expose events)
> 
> Drawing commands are byte-packed messages written to `data`. The command set includes: allocate image, free image, draw (composite), line, ellipse, string (draw text), load image data, read image data, etc. Each command is a single byte opcode followed by fixed-format arguments (image IDs, rectangles, points, colors).
> 
> This is *not* a pixel framebuffer. It's a **retained-mode imaging model**: you allocate named images on the server side, composite them with Porter-Duff operations, and the server handles clipping, display, and refresh. The same protocol works locally or over a network -- draw commands are just 9P writes.
> 
> Key abstractions:
> - **Image**: a rectangle of pixels in a given channel format (e.g., RGBA32, Grey8)
> - **Screen**: an image that tracks window stacking for refresh
> - **Font**: subfont-based rendering (subfonts cover Unicode ranges)
> 
> The draw device is described in `draw(3)` in the Plan 9 manual. The libdraw library provides a C API that writes to `/dev/draw`.
> 
> ### /dev/cons
> 
> `/dev/cons` is the console device. For a windowed process:
> - **Read**: blocks until the user types a line (cooked mode) or a character (raw mode). In cooked mode, the window system handles line editing (backspace, ^U, etc.) internally.
> - **Write**: appends text to the window's text buffer, which scrolls as needed.
> - `/dev/consctl`: write `rawon` to enter raw mode (each keystroke delivered immediately), `rawoff` to return to cooked mode.
> 
> This is the *only* text I/O interface a program needs. There is no termcap, no terminfo, no ANSI escape codes. A Plan 9 program that wants to be a text application just reads and writes `/dev/cons`. If it wants graphics, it uses `/dev/draw`. There is no hybrid horror like VT100 escape sequences trying to do cursor addressing in a character stream.
> 
> ## 4. sam and acme
> 
> ### sam (Rob Pike, 1987)
> 
> sam is a text editor with a unique architecture: **structural regular expressions** and a clean separation between front end and back end.
> 
> **Architecture**: sam runs as two processes connected by a byte-stream protocol (originally over an RS-232 line, later over a pipe or network). The back end (command interpreter) can run on a remote machine where the files live. The front end handles display and mouse interaction. This split predates and anticipates the language server protocol by decades.
> 
> **Command language**: sam's commands operate on **addresses** (ranges of text) using a composable language:
> 
> - Addresses: `.` (dot, current selection), `0` (start), `$` (end), `/regexp/`, line numbers, `#n` (character offset)
> - Compound addresses: `a1,a2` (range from a1 to a2), `a1+a2`, `a1-a2` (relative)
> - Commands: `a` (append), `i` (insert), `c` (change), `d` (delete), `s/pat/repl/` (substitute), `p` (print), `x/pat/cmd` (for each match, run cmd), `y/pat/cmd` (for each non-match), `g/pat/cmd` (conditional), `v/pat/cmd` (negative conditional)
> 
> **Structural regular expressions** (the key innovation): The `x` command extracts matches from the current selection and applies a command to each. This composes:
> 
> ```
> ,x/^[a-z]+:/ d
> ```
> This says: in the whole file (`,`), find each match of `/^[a-z]+:/` (`x`), and delete it (`d`). You can nest these:
> 
> ```
> ,x/^.*\n/ g/error/ p
> ```
> For every line in the file, if it contains "error", print it. This is grep reimplemented in the editor's command language. But unlike grep, it composes with arbitrary depth.
> 
> **Mouse integration**: sam uses a three-button mouse. Button 1 selects text. Button 2 opens a command menu. Button 3 searches for the selected text. This anticipates acme's model.
> 
> **Reference**: Rob Pike, "The Text Editor sam," Software - Practice and Experience, 17(11), 1987. Also: Rob Pike, "Structural Regular Expressions," EurOpen, 1987.
> 
> ### acme (Rob Pike, 1994)
> 
> acme is the culmination of Plan 9's text-as-interface philosophy. It is simultaneously a text editor, a window manager, a shell, and a programming environment. Its design is described in Pike's "Acme: A User Interface for Programmers" (1994).
> 
> **Screen layout**: The screen is divided into columns. Each column contains windows. Each window has:
> - A **tag line**: a line of text at the top containing the filename and editable command text
> - A **body**: the main text area
> - A **scrollbar**
> 
> The tag line is the key UI innovation. It typically reads something like:
> ```
> /home/user/file.c Del Snarf Get Put | Look
> ```
> The filename is there. The commands (Del, Snarf, Get, Put, Look) are just text. You can type any text into the tag and middle-click on it to execute it. You can add your own commands to any tag. The tag is not a widget -- it's editable text.
> 
> **Mouse chording** (three-button mouse, this is central):
> 
> - **Button 1 (left)**: Select text. Click to place cursor, sweep to select a range.
> - **Button 2 (middle)**: Execute. Select text (or click on a word) with B2 to run it as a command.
>   - Built-in commands (case-sensitive): `New`, `Del`, `Get`, `Put`, `Undo`, `Redo`, `Snarf`, `Paste`, `Look`, `Edit`, `Zerox`, `Font`, `Dump`, `Load`, `Exit`, `ID`, `Incl`, `Tab`, `Kill`, `Getall`, `Putall`
>   - Anything else is run as a shell command. Output appears in a `+Errors` window or a window named after the command.
>   - If the command is preceded by `<`, `>`, or `|`, it operates on the selection (pipe in, pipe out, or filter).
> - **Button 3 (right)**: Plumb / Look. B3-click on a filename opens it. B3 on `file.c:123` opens file.c at line 123. B3 on a word searches for the next occurrence. B3 behavior is extensible via the plumber.
> 
> **Chords** (pressing buttons in combination):
> - **B1 then B2** (1-2 chord): Cut the selected text
> - **B1 then B3** (1-3 chord): Paste, replacing the selection
> - **B2 then B1**: Add to the argument of the B2 command (extend selection for execution)
> 
> **Filesystem interface** (`/mnt/acme/`): This is how external programs control acme. Acme serves a 9P filesystem:
> 
> ```
> /mnt/acme/
>     index          — list of all windows (id, tag, etc.)
>     new/           — open this directory to create a new window
>         ctl        — write commands: name, dump, dumpdir, delete, clean, dirty, show, dot=addr
>         addr       — read/write the address (in sam address syntax)
>         body       — read/write the window body
>         data       — read/write text at the current address
>         tag        — read/write the tag line
>         event      — read/write mouse and keyboard events
>         errors     — the window's error output
>         xdata      — like data but with full rune (Unicode) handling
>     1/             — window with ID 1
>         ctl
>         addr
>         body
>         data
>         tag
>         event
>         ...
>     2/
>         ...
>     log            — stream of window events (create, delete, focus, etc.)
>     acme/          — (sometimes) the acme binary's own control
> ```
> 
> **The event file protocol**: Reading `/mnt/acme/N/event` yields events like:
> ```
> Mx x0 x1 flag length text
> ```
> Where M is the origin (keyboard K, mouse M, or external E), x is the type (execute X, look L, insert I, delete D), x0-x1 are character offsets, and text is the actual text. An external program can read events, handle them, and write back modified events (or pass them through unchanged). This lets programs intercept and extend acme's behavior.
> 
> Example: a mail program (`Mail`) runs as an external process. It creates acme windows for messages, reads events from those windows (user clicks on an address, user middle-clicks "Reply"), and performs appropriate actions. The mail program is not linked into acme -- it's a separate process communicating via the filesystem.
> 
> **The `Edit` command**: Middle-clicking `Edit` in a tag followed by a sam command runs that command on the window body. So acme incorporates sam's full structural regular expression language. You can type `Edit ,x/foo/c/bar/` in the tag, middle-click it, and perform a global substitution.
> 
> **Win**: The `win` command opens a new acme window that acts as a shell terminal. It's a trivial program -- it reads from the window's event file, feeds keyboard input to a shell, and writes shell output to the window body. The "terminal emulator" is just an acme client.
> 
> ## 5. The Plumber
> 
> The plumber (described in Rob Pike's "Plumbing and Other Utilities," 2000) is Plan 9's inter-application message router. It replaces hyperlinks, MIME handlers, drag-and-drop, and clipboard intelligence with a single mechanism: **pattern matching on text to route messages between applications**.
> 
> **Architecture**: The plumber is a file server that maintains named **ports**. Applications mount the plumber at `/mnt/plumb/` and see:
> 
> ```
> /mnt/plumb/
>     send       — write a plumb message here to route it
>     edit       — port for "open in editor" messages
>     web        — port for URLs
>     image      — port for image files
>     seemail    — port for incoming mail notifications
>     ...
> ```
> 
> An application that wants to handle a type of message opens the appropriate port file and reads from it. An application that wants to send a message writes to `send`.
> 
> **Message format**: A plumb message is a set of newline-terminated fields:
> ```
> src          — source application (e.g., "acme")
> dst          — destination port (e.g., "edit"), or empty for rule-based routing
> wdir         — working directory for relative paths
> type         — data type (usually "text")
> attr         — key=value attributes (e.g., "addr=13")
> ndata        — byte count of the data
> data         — the actual data (e.g., a filename, URL, error message)
> ```
> 
> **Plumbing rules** (in `$HOME/lib/plumbing` or `/usr/$user/lib/plumbing`): Rules match messages by pattern and route them to ports or launch programs. Each rule has:
> 
> ```
> # Match compiler error messages like "file.c:42: error"
> type is text
> data matches '([a-zA-Z¡-￿_./\-]+):([0-9]+)([:, ]|$)'
> arg isfile $1
> plumb to edit
> plumb client $editor
> attr add addr=$2
> ```
> 
> Rule components:
> - `type is ...` — match the message type
> - `data matches 'regexp'` — match the data against a regex; captured groups become `$1`, `$2`, etc.
> - `arg isfile path` — verify that a path actually exists as a file
> - `arg isdir path` — verify directory existence
> - `plumb to port` — route to this port
> - `plumb start command` — launch a program if no one is listening on the port
> - `plumb client program` — preferred client for this port
> - `attr add key=value` — add attributes to the message
> 
> **How it works in practice**: In acme, you right-click (B3) on the text `parse.c:157`. Acme sends a plumb message with data `parse.c:157` and wdir set to the directory of the current window. The plumber matches this against its rules, finds the rule for `filename:linenumber` patterns, verifies `parse.c` exists, and routes the message to the `edit` port with attribute `addr=157`. Acme (listening on `edit`) receives the message and opens `parse.c` at line 157.
> 
> This same mechanism handles:
> - URLs (route to web browser)
> - Man page references like `printf(3)` (route to man page viewer)
> - Mail addresses (route to mail composer)
> - Image filenames (route to image viewer)
> - Compiler errors in any format (configurable patterns)
> 
> The plumber is completely user-extensible. Add a rule, and any text matching that pattern becomes "clickable" everywhere in the system -- not just in one application, but in every application that sends plumb messages.
> 
> ## 6. Design Philosophy: Text-as-Interface and Filesystem-as-IPC
> 
> The Plan 9 approach rests on two interlocking principles:
> 
> ### Text-as-Interface
> 
> Text is the universal data type because:
> 1. It's human-readable without tools
> 2. It's composable -- you can select, copy, edit, pipe, and transform it with standard tools
> 3. It's self-describing in many contexts (a filename looks like a filename, a URL looks like a URL)
> 4. It requires no serialization/deserialization infrastructure
> 
> In Plan 9's UI, there is no distinction between "display text" and "actionable text." All text is potentially actionable. The action is determined by context (which mouse button, which plumbing rules) rather than by the text's provenance (which widget it's in, which application rendered it).
> 
> This collapses the conventional GUI distinction between:
> - Labels (display only) and buttons (actionable) → all text is both
> - Output (read only) and input (editable) → all text is editable
> - Commands (typed at a prompt) and results (displayed below) → both are in the same text buffer
> 
> ### Filesystem-as-IPC
> 
> Plan 9 uses synthetic filesystems as the universal IPC mechanism. Where Unix uses:
> - Signals, ptrace, /proc (process control)
> - X11/Wayland protocols (graphics)
> - D-Bus, XDG portals (desktop integration)
> - MIME handlers, file associations (content routing)
> - Clipboard protocols (data exchange)
> 
> Plan 9 uses one mechanism: **a process serves a filesystem, and clients interact by reading and writing files**. The protocol is always 9P. This means:
> 
> 1. **Uniform interface**: Every service looks like a filesystem. You learn one model.
> 2. **Network transparency**: 9P works over any transport. Mount a remote machine's `/dev/draw` and you can display your windows there.
> 3. **Composability**: Mount one file server's output as another's input. rio is a draw client and a draw server simultaneously.
> 4. **Scriptability**: Any operation that can be done interactively can be done from a shell script by reading and writing files. `echo delete > /dev/wctl` closes a window.
> 5. **Debuggability**: `cat /dev/window` shows you the current window rectangle. `cat /mnt/acme/index` lists all acme windows. The state is always inspectable.
> 
> The key difference from Unix's "everything is a file" (which really means "everything is a file descriptor"): Plan 9 means it literally. The filesystem namespace is the service directory. `/dev/draw` isn't an ioctl-laden character device -- it's a directory of files with documented read/write semantics.
> 
> ## 7. Influence and Legacy
> 
> ### plan9port (Russ Cox)
> 
> plan9port (aka Plan 9 from User Space) ports the Plan 9 userland to Unix systems (Linux, macOS, BSDs). Available at https://9fans.github.io/plan9port/ and https://github.com/9fans/plan9port.
> 
> It includes:
> - **acme** — fully functional, the most actively used component. Many working programmers use plan9port acme daily on macOS/Linux.
> - **sam** — the editor
> - **plumber** — works on Unix, routes messages between plan9port applications
> - **9P library (lib9p, libixp)** — for writing 9P servers in C
> - **rio** — a port exists but is less commonly used (requires specific X11 setup)
> - **devdraw** — a Unix process that emulates `/dev/draw` as a separate process communicating via a pipe (since Unix lacks the kernel draw device)
> - **fontsrv** — serves system fonts in Plan 9 subfont format
> 
> The plan9port acme ecosystem has spawned tools like:
> - **acme-lsp** — Language Server Protocol bridge for acme
> - **A** — acme wrapper scripts
> - **Watch** — re-run commands on file changes (like entr, but acme-native)
> - **editinacme** — `$EDITOR` that opens files in a running acme instance
> 
> ### 9fans community
> 
> The 9fans mailing list (now archived, historically at 9fans@9fans.net) was the primary community. Many ideas from Plan 9 have leaked into wider systems culture:
> 
> - **UTF-8**: Invented by Ken Thompson and Rob Pike for Plan 9 in 1992. Now the dominant character encoding worldwide.
> - **/proc filesystem**: Plan 9's /proc (process-as-directory with ctl, status, mem files) influenced Linux's /proc, though Linux's version is more ad hoc.
> - **FUSE**: Filesystem in Userspace was directly inspired by Plan 9's user-level file servers. 9P clients (v9fs) exist in the Linux kernel.
> - **Factotum/secstore**: Plan 9's authentication agent influenced ssh-agent and similar tools. The separation of authentication from applications is now standard.
> - **Namespaces**: Plan 9's per-process namespaces are the ancestor of Linux mount namespaces (and by extension, containers).
> - **Inferno/Limbo**: A commercial descendant of Plan 9 with a virtual machine. Limbo's channels influenced Go's channels.
> - **Go**: Created by Rob Pike, Ken Thompson, and Robert Griesemer. Go's goroutines and channels descend from Plan 9's Alef language and CSP heritage. The `io.Reader`/`io.Writer` interfaces echo Plan 9's everything-is-a-file-read/write model.
> 
> ### Tools directly inspired by Plan 9
> 
> - **wmii** and **i3**: Tiling window managers influenced by acme's column layout
> - **wily**: An acme clone for Unix (predating plan9port)
> - **vis editor**: Combines sam's structural regular expressions with vi-like modal editing
> - **kakoune**: Editor that adopted sam's structural regular expressions (the `x/pattern/` extraction model)
> - **Russ Cox's work on re2 and Go regexp**: The regexp engine descended from Plan 9's libregexp
>

>

> 2. Rob Pike, "Acme: A User Interface for Programmers," USENIX, 1994 — https://9p.io/sys/doc/acme.html
> 3. Rob Pike, "The Text Editor sam," Software - Practice and Experience, 17(11), 1987 — https://9p.io/sys/doc/sam/sam.html
> 4. Rob Pike, "Plumbing and Other Utilities," 2000 — https://9p.io/sys/doc/plumb.html
> 5. Rob Pike, "Rio: Design of a Concurrent Window System," 2000 — https://9p.io/sys/doc/rio.html
> 6. Plan 9 manual pages — https://9p.io/magic/man2html (especially draw(3), cons(3), rio(1), acme(1), plumber(4))
> 7. Plan 9 from User Space — https://9fans.github.io/plan9port/
> 8. Rob Pike, "Structural Regular Expressions," EurOpen, 1987
> 9. Russ Cox, "Plan 9 from User Space," https://github.com/9fans/plan9port
> 10. Ken Thompson & Rob Pike, "Hello World, or Καλημέρα κόσμε, or こんにちは世界" (UTF-8 paper), USENIX, 1993
>

I was unable to fetch the live pages (WebFetch was denied), so this is from training data. The technical details (file paths, protocol formats, command names, mouse button semantics) are accurate to the Plan 9 4th edition and plan9port sources, but if you need verbatim quotes from a specific paper, I'd need web access to pull those.
> 
> The areas where my confidence is highest: acme's filesystem interface, the plumber message format, sam's command language, and the /dev/draw interface -- these are precisely documented in man pages and papers I've absorbed thoroughly. ### `2026-03-15T00:57:59.265Z`

> Propose a new change - create the change and generate all artifacts in one step.
> 
> I'll create a change with artifacts:
> - proposal.md (what & why)
> - design.md (how)
> - tasks.md (implementation steps)
> 
> When ready to implement, run /opsx:apply
> 
> ---
> 
> **Input**: The argument after `/opsx:propose` is the change name (kebab-case), OR a description of what the user wants to build.
> 
> **Steps**
> 
> 1. **If no input provided, ask what they want to build**
> 
>    Use the **AskUserQuestion tool** (open-ended, no preset options) to ask:
>    > "What change do you want to work on? Describe what you want to build or fix."
> 
>    From their description, derive a kebab-case name (e.g., "add user authentication" → `add-user-auth`).
> 
>    **IMPORTANT**: Do NOT proceed without understanding what the user wants to build.
> 
> 2. **Create the change directory**
>    ```bash
>    openspec new change "<name>"
>    ```
>    This creates a scaffolded change at `openspec/changes/<name>/` with `.openspec.yaml`.
> 
> 3. **Get the artifact build order**
>    ```bash
>    openspec status --change "<name>" --json
>    ```
>    Parse the JSON to get:
>    - `applyRequires`: array of artifact IDs needed before implementation (e.g., `["tasks"]`)
>    - `artifacts`: list of all artifacts with their status and dependencies
> 
> 4. **Create artifacts in sequence until apply-ready**
> 
>    Use the **TodoWrite tool** to track progress through the artifacts.
> 
>    Loop through artifacts in dependency order (artifacts with no pending dependencies first):
> 
>    a. **For each artifact that is `ready` (dependencies satisfied)**:
>       - Get instructions:
>         ```bash
>         openspec instructions <artifact-id> --change "<name>" --json
>         ```
>       - The instructions JSON includes:
>         - `context`: Project background (constraints for you - do NOT include in output)
>         - `rules`: Artifact-specific rules (constraints for you - do NOT include in output)
>         - `template`: The structure to use for your output file
>         - `instruction`: Schema-specific guidance for this artifact type
>         - `outputPath`: Where to write the artifact
>         - `dependencies`: Completed artifacts to read for context
>       - Read any completed dependency files for context
>       - Create the artifact file using `template` as the structure
>       - Apply `context` and `rules` as constraints - but do NOT copy them into the file
>       - Show brief progress: "Created <artifact-id>"
> 
>    b. **Continue until all `applyRequires` artifacts are complete**
>       - After creating each artifact, re-run `openspec status --change "<name>" --json`
>       - Check if every artifact ID in `applyRequires` has `status: "done"` in the artifacts array
>       - Stop when all `applyRequires` artifacts are done
> 
>    c. **If an artifact requires user input** (unclear context):
>       - Use **AskUserQuestion tool** to clarify
>       - Then continue with creation
> 
> 5. **Show final status**
>    ```bash
>    openspec status --change "<name>"
>    ```
> 
> **Output**
> 
> After completing all artifacts, summarize:
> - Change name and location
> - List of artifacts created with brief descriptions
> - What's ready: "All artifacts created! Ready for implementation."
> - Prompt: "Run `/opsx:apply` to start implementing."
> 
> **Artifact Creation Guidelines**
> 
> - Follow the `instruction` field from `openspec instructions` for each artifact type
> - The schema defines what each artifact should contain - follow it
> - Read dependency artifacts for context before creating new ones
> - Use `template` as the structure for your output file - fill in its sections
> - **IMPORTANT**: `context` and `rules` are constraints for YOU, not content for the file
>   - Do NOT copy `<context>`, `<rules>`, `<project_context>` blocks into the artifact
>   - These guide what you write, but should never appear in the output
> 
> **Guardrails**
> - Create ALL artifacts needed for implementation (as defined by schema's `apply.requires`)
> - Always read dependency artifacts before creating a new one
> - If context is critically unclear, ask the user - but prefer making reasonable decisions to keep momentum
> - If a change with that name already exists, ask if user wants to continue it or create a new one
> - Verify each artifact file exists after writing before proceeding to next
> 
> 
> ARGUMENTS: pane-proto foundation crate

### `2026-03-15T01:08:43.256Z`

> <command-message>opsx:apply</command-message>
> <command-name>/opsx:apply</command-name>

### `2026-03-15T01:08:43.256Z`

> Implement tasks from an OpenSpec change.
> 
> **Input**: Optionally specify a change name (e.g., `/opsx:apply add-auth`). If omitted, check if it can be inferred from conversation context. If vague or ambiguous you MUST prompt for available changes.
> 
> **Steps**
> 
> 1. **Select the change**
> 
>    If a name is provided, use it. Otherwise:
>    - Infer from conversation context if the user mentioned a change
>    - Auto-select if only one active change exists
>    - If ambiguous, run `openspec list --json` to get available changes and use the **AskUserQuestion tool** to let the user select
> 
>    Always announce: "Using change: <name>" and how to override (e.g., `/opsx:apply <other>`).
> 
> 2. **Check status to understand the schema**
>    ```bash
>    openspec status --change "<name>" --json
>    ```
>    Parse the JSON to understand:
>    - `schemaName`: The workflow being used (e.g., "spec-driven")
>    - Which artifact contains the tasks (typically "tasks" for spec-driven, check status for others)
> 
> 3. **Get apply instructions**
> 
>    ```bash
>    openspec instructions apply --change "<name>" --json
>    ```
> 
>    This returns:
>    - Context file paths (varies by schema)
>    - Progress (total, complete, remaining)
>    - Task list with status
>    - Dynamic instruction based on current state
> 
>    **Handle states:**
>    - If `state: "blocked"` (missing artifacts): show message, suggest using `/opsx:continue`
>    - If `state: "all_done"`: congratulate, suggest archive
>    - Otherwise: proceed to implementation
> 
> 4. **Read context files**
> 
>    Read the files listed in `contextFiles` from the apply instructions output.
>    The files depend on the schema being used:
>    - **spec-driven**: proposal, specs, design, tasks
>    - Other schemas: follow the contextFiles from CLI output
> 
> 5. **Show current progress**
> 
>    Display:
>    - Schema being used
>    - Progress: "N/M tasks complete"
>    - Remaining tasks overview
>    - Dynamic instruction from CLI
> 
> 6. **Implement tasks (loop until done or blocked)**
> 
>    For each pending task:
>    - Show which task is being worked on
>    - Make the code changes required
>    - Keep changes minimal and focused
>    - Mark task complete in the tasks file: `- [ ]` → `- [x]`
>    - Continue to next task
> 
>    **Pause if:**
>    - Task is unclear → ask for clarification
>    - Implementation reveals a design issue → suggest updating artifacts
>    - Error or blocker encountered → report and wait for guidance
>    - User interrupts
> 
> 7. **On completion or pause, show status**
> 
>    Display:
>    - Tasks completed this session
>    - Overall progress: "N/M tasks complete"
>    - If all done: suggest archive
>    - If paused: explain why and wait for guidance
> 
> **Output During Implementation**
> 
> ```
> ## Implementing: <change-name> (schema: <schema-name>)
> 
> Working on task 3/7: <task description>
> [...implementation happening...]
> ✓ Task complete
> 
> Working on task 4/7: <task description>
> [...implementation happening...]
> ✓ Task complete
> ```
> 
> **Output On Completion**
> 
> ```
> ## Implementation Complete
> 
> **Change:** <change-name>
> **Schema:** <schema-name>
> **Progress:** 7/7 tasks complete ✓
> 
> ### Completed This Session
> - [x] Task 1
> - [x] Task 2
> ...
> 
> All tasks complete! You can archive this change with `/opsx:archive`.
> ```
> 
> **Output On Pause (Issue Encountered)**
> 
> ```
> ## Implementation Paused
> 
> **Change:** <change-name>
> **Schema:** <schema-name>
> **Progress:** 4/7 tasks complete
> 
> ### Issue Encountered
> <description of the issue>
> 
> **Options:**
> 1. <option 1>
> 2. <option 2>
> 3. Other approach
> 
> What would you like to do?
> ```
> 
> **Guardrails**
> - Keep going through tasks until done or blocked
> - Always read context files before starting (from the apply instructions output)
> - If task is ambiguous, pause and ask before implementing
> - If implementation reveals issues, pause and suggest artifact updates
> - Keep code changes minimal and scoped to each task
> - Update task checkbox immediately after completing each task
> - Pause on errors, blockers, or unclear requirements - don't guess
> - Use contextFiles from CLI output, don't assume specific file names
> 
> **Fluid Workflow Integration**
> 
> This skill supports the "actions on a change" model:
> 
> - **Can be invoked anytime**: Before all artifacts are done (if tasks exist), after partial implementation, interleaved with other actions
> - **Allows artifact updates**: If implementation reveals design issues, suggest updating artifacts - not phase-locked, work fluidly
