### `2026-03-17T23:14:50.324Z`

> yes continue. one thing im curious about is overengineering: should we not consider that we can use libraries from other terminal emulators in our task? the only place pane-shell would depart from that is in providing textual interfaces that have richer features than a normal terminal editor. emacs is sort of like that; in essence, this is like if we were to do "emacs as an os" but from an interface-agnostic perspective, via well thought and statically typed abstractions rather than the wild west of lisp engineering

### `2026-03-17T23:14:57.180Z`

### `2026-03-17T23:16:28.751Z`

> yes continue. one thing im curious about is overengineering: should we not consider that we can use libraries from other terminal emulators in our task? the only place pane-shell would depart from that is in providing textual interfaces that have richer features than a normal terminal editor. emacs is sort of like that; in essence, the ux is like if we were to do "emacs as an os" but from an interface-agnostic perspective, via well thought and statically typed abstractions rather than the wild west of lisp engineering. the metaphor connects to user tooling as well (building off BeOS's translation kit and replicants): there should be a way to modularly compose modifications of the behavior of the codebase, given in a declarative specification (with good ux abstractions for modifying it in various ways as suits user preference). I imagine that there could be a vast ecosystem of plugin design over the surface of (safe parts of) the OS itself

> the ux philosophy can be summed up as aspiring to the ideal that "pane is about expressive ways to composes ideas". and deeper, pane is about the strength of its own specification, a small core that all of the actual ux is built from by first principles, which can in principle be modified. We do not shy away from the possibilities, we give guard rails for inexperienced users and a ladder for them to climb as they become power users

### `2026-03-18T05:47:34.793Z`

> do some research into the Haiku API and how the app roster works in their system, and then engage me on this question. in fact take a look the whole Haiku API/Kit infrastructure and get back to me

### `2026-03-18T06:47:19.824Z`

> I think I like this distinction -- perhaps we should write a pane-init as an abstraction layer over particular choices of init systems; all pane cares about are the interfaces it exposes to execute its intended operational semantics, and our criteria for support can be determined by contractual guarantees such frameworks satisfy that are sufficient for what we want to do in pane. for 2: i dont understand this distinction, I think that its implied that the former arises after the latter in practice, that is, some sequence of events happens that leads to roster starting a new process. the point is to establish an ecology of system workflows which are robust and economical. Message passing frameworks may be a source of nondeterministic behavior, but they were a genuine advancement in the 90s inasmuch as they forced the operational semantics of the OS to implement routines for handling operations in the abstract, and to be able to have sensible decision procedures for each little component of a task which does not need to account for the global state as a whole (only those areas for which sideeffects result) -- managing similar system complexity in a monolithic architecture is a complete nightmare. The idea is you establish a protocol, ground rules, and then hope the resulting system is capable of sutatined operation in the face of emergent system complexity. This seems very risky, but the startling fact was that it worked! BeOS was a highly stable OS leagues ahead of Windows 95 and MacOS 7; it could play 30 videos simultaneously on a pentium 3 processor _and still be eagerly responsive to user input_, without a hint of instability. That was because it was forward looking -- the first system to understand the importance of media (hence it had a media kit) for the user experience. Mime types were baked in from the start (they clearly recognized the importance of metadata by what one observes about their design of BeFS). BeOS was engineered to be most efficient symmetric multiprocessing frameworks (when all but supercomputers were single-threaded cpu systems, it experimented with the BeBox having two (IIRC) processors for the consumer market). This bet is what led to the importance the BeAPI placed on multithreading, which allowed for a very principled scheduler which was able to manage system processes at a very fine grained level, but this lead to a superior UX even on single-core systems. Certainly designing the system by construction for parallelism would be most optimal for multi-core systems, but it also turned out that this is because overcoming the formidible challenges could only be accomplished by superior systems design.

### `2026-03-18T06:54:55.314Z`

> yes, and also lets apply my meditation to clarify the overall specification of pane, as I think this was a great chance for me to articulate the specific influence and insight I have into why these systems were a joy to use (I used BeOS myself in fact -- even after Be Inc dissolved, along with a small community which ended up coalescing around Haiku)

### `2026-03-18T07:03:50.392Z`

> Now one more aspect, while sequent calculus can be useful, I think the kind of framework we should be thinking towards is session types. Message passing was effective for the reasons I described, but the untyped nature of C++ could not be fully reocnciled (although due to the quality of the system, in practice the BeAPI was the most stable OS api of its time and indeed many years beyond, especially considering it was at least as advanced as windows xp by the time the latter came out). So we know operationally, enough good design patterns correlate with message passing that a system founded on them is viable and indeed, compelling. Now we have type systems that more rigorously specify the behavior of such systems like session types, so we can leverage the wisdom of more than two decades of theoretical development that eventually *caught up* to what they were doing by skill, sensibility, and intuition alone

### `2026-03-18T07:17:31.538Z`

> yes we should. actually I think that we should really look at session types as the theoretical foundation for our impelementation. I think an excellent place to start is integrating this library https://github.com/faiface/par

### `2026-03-18T07:54:18.486Z`

> <command-message>opsx:propose</command-message>
> <command-name>/opsx:propose</command-name>
> <command-args>we need to do a tightening of our whole specification for clarity, accuracy, precision, as well as comprehensively review all the design references: haiku/beos, plan9, session types with respect to our vision and refine our planning materials to begin this project on firm footing</command-args>

### `2026-03-18T07:54:18.486Z`

> Propose a new change - create the change and generate all artifacts in one step.
> 
> I'll create a change with artifacts:
> - proposal.md (what & why)
> - design.md (how)
> - tasks.md (implementation steps)
> 
> When ready to implement, run /opsx:apply
> 
> ---
> 
> **Input**: The argument after `/opsx:propose` is the change name (kebab-case), OR a description of what the user wants to build.
> 
> **Steps**
> 
> 1. **If no input provided, ask what they want to build**
> 
>    Use the **AskUserQuestion tool** (open-ended, no preset options) to ask:
>    > "What change do you want to work on? Describe what you want to build or fix."
> 
>    From their description, derive a kebab-case name (e.g., "add user authentication" → `add-user-auth`).
> 
>    **IMPORTANT**: Do NOT proceed without understanding what the user wants to build.
> 
> 2. **Create the change directory**
>    ```bash
>    openspec new change "<name>"
>    ```
>    This creates a scaffolded change at `openspec/changes/<name>/` with `.openspec.yaml`.
> 
> 3. **Get the artifact build order**
>    ```bash
>    openspec status --change "<name>" --json
>    ```
>    Parse the JSON to get:
>    - `applyRequires`: array of artifact IDs needed before implementation (e.g., `["tasks"]`)
>    - `artifacts`: list of all artifacts with their status and dependencies
> 
> 4. **Create artifacts in sequence until apply-ready**
> 
>    Use the **TodoWrite tool** to track progress through the artifacts.
> 
>    Loop through artifacts in dependency order (artifacts with no pending dependencies first):
> 
>    a. **For each artifact that is `ready` (dependencies satisfied)**:
>       - Get instructions:
>         ```bash
>         openspec instructions <artifact-id> --change "<name>" --json
>         ```
>       - The instructions JSON includes:
>         - `context`: Project background (constraints for you - do NOT include in output)
>         - `rules`: Artifact-specific rules (constraints for you - do NOT include in output)
>         - `template`: The structure to use for your output file
>         - `instruction`: Schema-specific guidance for this artifact type
>         - `outputPath`: Where to write the artifact
>         - `dependencies`: Completed artifacts to read for context
>       - Read any completed dependency files for context
>       - Create the artifact file using `template` as the structure
>       - Apply `context` and `rules` as constraints - but do NOT copy them into the file
>       - Show brief progress: "Created <artifact-id>"
> 
>    b. **Continue until all `applyRequires` artifacts are complete**
>       - After creating each artifact, re-run `openspec status --change "<name>" --json`
>       - Check if every artifact ID in `applyRequires` has `status: "done"` in the artifacts array
>       - Stop when all `applyRequires` artifacts are done
> 
>    c. **If an artifact requires user input** (unclear context):
>       - Use **AskUserQuestion tool** to clarify
>       - Then continue with creation
> 
> 5. **Show final status**
>    ```bash
>    openspec status --change "<name>"
>    ```
> 
> **Output**
> 
> After completing all artifacts, summarize:
> - Change name and location
> - List of artifacts created with brief descriptions
> - What's ready: "All artifacts created! Ready for implementation."
> - Prompt: "Run `/opsx:apply` to start implementing."
> 
> **Artifact Creation Guidelines**
> 
> - Follow the `instruction` field from `openspec instructions` for each artifact type
> - The schema defines what each artifact should contain - follow it
> - Read dependency artifacts for context before creating new ones
> - Use `template` as the structure for your output file - fill in its sections
> - **IMPORTANT**: `context` and `rules` are constraints for YOU, not content for the file
>   - Do NOT copy `<context>`, `<rules>`, `<project_context>` blocks into the artifact
>   - These guide what you write, but should never appear in the output
> 
> **Guardrails**
> - Create ALL artifacts needed for implementation (as defined by schema's `apply.requires`)
> - Always read dependency artifacts before creating a new one
> - If context is critically unclear, ask the user - but prefer making reasonable decisions to keep momentum
> - If a change with that name already exists, ask if user wants to continue it or create a new one
> - Verify each artifact file exists after writing before proceeding to next
> 
> 
> ARGUMENTS: we need to do a tightening of our whole specification for clarity, accuracy, precision, as well as comprehensively review all the design references: haiku/beos, plan9, session types with respect to our vision and refine our planning materials to begin this project on firm footing
